<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41467"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The asparagus genome sheds light on the origin and evolution of a young Y chromosome"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Communications 2017 8:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2017-11-02"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2017 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Sex chromosomes evolved from autosomes many times across the eukaryote phylogeny. Several models have been proposed to explain this transition, some involving male and female sterility mutations linked in a region of suppressed recombination between X and Y (or Z/W, U/V) chromosomes. Comparative and experimental analysis of a reference genome assembly for a double haploid YY male garden asparagus (Asparagus officinalis L.) individual implicates separate but linked genes as responsible for sex determination. Dioecy has evolved recently within Asparagus and sex chromosomes are cytogenetically identical with the Y, harboring a megabase segment that is missing from the X. We show that deletion of this entire region results in a male-to-female conversion, whereas loss of a single suppressor of female development drives male-to-hermaphrodite conversion. A single copy anther-specific gene with a male sterile Arabidopsis knockout phenotype is also in the Y-specific region, supporting a two-gene model for sex chromosome evolution. Several models have been proposed to explain the emergence of sex chromosomes. Here, through comparative genomics and mutant analysis, Harkess et al. show that linked but separate genes on the Y chromosome are responsible for sex determination in Asparagus, supporting a two-gene model for sex chromosome evolution."/>

Here, through comparative genomics and mutant analysis, Harkess et al. show that linked but separate genes on the Y chromosome are responsible for sex determination in Asparagus, supporting a two-gene model for sex chromosome evolution."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Sex chromosomes evolved from autosomes many times across the eukaryote phylogeny. Several models have been proposed to explain this transition, some involving male and female sterility mutations linked in a region of suppressed recombination between <i>X</i> and <i>Y</i> (or <i>Z</i>/<i>W</i>, <i>U</i>/<i>V</i>) chromosomes. Comparative and experimental analysis of a reference genome assembly for a double haploid <i>YY</i> male garden asparagus (<i>Asparagus officinalis</i> L.) individual implicates separate but linked genes as responsible for sex determination. Dioecy has evolved recently within <i>Asparagus</i> and sex chromosomes are cytogenetically identical with the <i>Y</i>, harboring a megabase segment that is missing from the <i>X</i>. We show that deletion of this entire region results in a male-to-female conversion, whereas loss of a single suppressor of female development drives male-to-hermaphrodite conversion. A single copy anther-specific gene with a male sterile <i>Arabidopsis</i> knockout phenotype is also in the <i>Y-</i>specific region, supporting a two-gene model for sex chromosome evolution.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Introduction"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Introduction</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Over the last century, cytological and genetic evidence has implicated sex chromosomes as controlling sex determination in many diecious species across plants, animals, and fungi<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Morgan, T. H. Heredity and sex. Z. Indukt. Abstamm. Vererbungsl. &#xA;                           12, 159â161 (1914)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d43926e1362">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Morgan, T. H. Recent results relating to chromosomes and genetics. Q. Rev. Biol. &#xA;                           1, 186 (1926)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d43926e1365">2</a></sup> but much remains unclear concerning their origin and early evolution from autosomes. Whereas the concomitant origins of separate sexes and sex chromosomes are ancient in metazoan lineages, the evolution of dioecy has occurred independently many times in the evolutionary history of flowering plants, making angiosperms ideal systems for investigating the evolution of sex chromosomes across various time scales<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Ming, R., Bendahmane, A. &amp; Renner, S. S. Sex chromosomes in land plants. Annu. Rev. Plant. Biol. &#xA;                           62, 485â514 (2011)." href="#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d43926e1369">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Charlesworth, B. &amp; Charlesworth, D. A model for the evolution of dioecy and gynodioecy. Am. Nat. &#xA;                           112, 975â997 (1978)." href="#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d43926e1369_1">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Charlesworth, D. &amp; Charlesworth, B. Population genetics of partial male-sterility and the evolution of monoecy and dioecy. Heredity &#xA;                           41, 137â153 (1978)." href="#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d43926e1369_2">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Charlesworth, D. Plant contributions to our understanding of sex chromosome evolution. New. Phytol. &#xA;                           208, 52â65 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d43926e1372">6</a></sup>. Roughly 6% of all angiosperms are dioecious<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Renner, S. S. The relative and absolute frequencies of angiosperm sexual systems: dioecy, monoecy, gynodioecy, and an updated online database. Am. J. Bot. &#xA;                           101, 1588â1596 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d43926e1376">7</a></sup> but the genes controlling sex determination and their organization on sex chromosomes in diecious plants are largely unknown.</p><p>On the basis of his own work in <i>Silene latifolia</i> (formerly <i>Melandrium album</i>) and a review of data from other diecious plant systems including garden asparagus, Mogens Westergaard posited in 1958 that the evolution of an active <i>Y</i> chromosome from an autosome involved at least two genes: a dominant suppressor of female function and a gene essential for male function that is missing from the <i>X</i> chromosome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Westergaard, M. The mechanism of sex determination in dioecious flowering plants. Adv. Genet. &#xA;                           9, 217â281 (1958)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d43926e1395">8</a></sup>. This two-gene hypothesis was later advanced by Brian and Deborah Charlesworth in an evolutionary model for the conversion of an autosomal chromosome pair to sex chromosomes in association with a transition from hermaphroditism to dioecy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Charlesworth, B. &amp; Charlesworth, D. A model for the evolution of dioecy and gynodioecy. Am. Nat. &#xA;                           112, 975â997 (1978)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d43926e1400">4</a></sup>. Alternatively, a single-sex determination gene could dominantly repress female development and promote male function. Recently, Boualem et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Boualem, A. et al. A cucurbit androecy gene reveals how unisexual flowers develop and dioecy emerges. Science &#xA;                           350, 688â691 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d43926e1404">9</a></sup> have elegantly engineered a transition from monoecy to dioecy in melon (<i>Cucumis melo</i>) by selecting on natural variation to synthesize a population that is fixed for a null form of the feminizing gene <i>CmACS11</i> and segregating for a functional <i>C2H2</i> zinc finger transcription factor gene, <i>WIP1</i>, that has been shown to suppress both carpel development and stamen development. As had been demonstrated earlier in maize<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Westergaard, M. The mechanism of sex determination in dioecious flowering plants. Adv. Genet. &#xA;                           9, 217â281 (1958)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d43926e1421">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Jones, D. F. Unisexual maize plants and their bearing on sex differentiation in other plants and in animals. Genetics &#xA;                           19, 552â567 (1934)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d43926e1424">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Dellaporta, S. L. &amp; Calderon-Urrea, A. The sex determination process in maize. Science &#xA;                           266, 1501â1505 (1994)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d43926e1427">11</a></sup>, fixation of a null mutation at one locus in a monecious population set the stage for segregation of a null mutation at a second locus, resulting in dioecy and single-gene sex determination. Indeed the experimental one-gene sex determination systems described for melon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Boualem, A. et al. A cucurbit androecy gene reveals how unisexual flowers develop and dioecy emerges. Science &#xA;                           350, 688â691 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d43926e1431">9</a></sup> and maize<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Westergaard, M. The mechanism of sex determination in dioecious flowering plants. Adv. Genet. &#xA;                           9, 217â281 (1958)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d43926e1435">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Jones, D. F. Unisexual maize plants and their bearing on sex differentiation in other plants and in animals. Genetics &#xA;                           19, 552â567 (1934)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d43926e1438">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Dellaporta, S. L. &amp; Calderon-Urrea, A. The sex determination process in maize. Science &#xA;                           266, 1501â1505 (1994)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d43926e1441">11</a></sup> involve mutations in two genes, but it is the segregation of a single functional gene that ultimately determines sex. A single non-coding RNA has also been hypothesized as a sex determination gene in persimmons (<i>Diospyros</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Akagi, T., Henry, I. M., Tao, R. &amp; Comai, L. A Y-chromosomeâencoded small RNA acts as a sex determinant in persimmons. Science &#xA;                           346, 646â650 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d43926e1448">12</a></sup>. It is important to note that one- and two-gene models for the origin of dioecy and sex determination are not mutually exclusive explanations for the numerous independent origins of dioecy and sex chromosomes across the angiosperms<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Renner, S. S. Pathways for making unisexual flowers and unisexual plants: moving beyond theâ¯âTwo mutations linked on one chromosomeâ model. Am. J. Bot. &#xA;                           103, 587â589 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d43926e1452">13</a></sup>.</p><p>Here we directly test the one- and two-gene hypotheses for the origin of sex determination in the genus <i>Asparagus</i>, using garden asparagus (<i>Asparagus officinalis</i> L.) as a representative of the derived diecious clade within the otherwise hermaphroditic genus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Kubota, S., Konno, I. &amp; Kanno, A. Molecular phylogeny of the genus Asparagus (Asparagaceae) explains interspecific crossability between the garden asparagus (A. officinalis) and other Asparagus species. Theor. Appl. Genet. &#xA;                           124, 345â354 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d43926e1465">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Norup, M. F. et al. Evolution of Asparagus L. (Asparagaceae): Out-of-South-Africa and multiple origins of sexual dimorphism. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. &#xA;                           92, 25â44 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d43926e1468">15</a></sup>. Phylogenetic analyses suggest that dioecy evolved once or potentially twice within a single clade in the <i>Asparagus</i> phylogeny<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Kubota, S., Konno, I. &amp; Kanno, A. Molecular phylogeny of the genus Asparagus (Asparagaceae) explains interspecific crossability between the garden asparagus (A. officinalis) and other Asparagus species. Theor. Appl. Genet. &#xA;                           124, 345â354 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d43926e1475">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Norup, M. F. et al. Evolution of Asparagus L. (Asparagaceae): Out-of-South-Africa and multiple origins of sexual dimorphism. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. &#xA;                           92, 25â44 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d43926e1478">15</a></sup>, coincident with increases in genome size and repetitive DNA content<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Harkess, A. et al. Retrotransposon proliferation coincident with the evolution of dioecy in Asparagus. G3: Genes Genomes Genet. &#xA;                           6, 2679â2685 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d43926e1483">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Å tajner, N., Bohanec, B. &amp; Javornik, B. Genetic variability of economically important Asparagus species as revealed by genome size analysis and rDNA ITS polymorphisms. Plant Sci. &#xA;                           162, 931â937 (2002)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d43926e1486">17</a></sup>, as well as a range expansion from southern Africa into northern Africa and Europe<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Kubota, S., Konno, I. &amp; Kanno, A. Molecular phylogeny of the genus Asparagus (Asparagaceae) explains interspecific crossability between the garden asparagus (A. officinalis) and other Asparagus species. Theor. Appl. Genet. &#xA;                           124, 345â354 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d43926e1490">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="A. Kanno, J. Yokoyama, âAsparagusâ in Wild Crop Relatives: Genomic and Breeding Resources (ed C. Kole) Chapter 3 (Springer-Verlag Berlin Heidelberg, 2011)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d43926e1493">18</a></sup>. In garden asparagus, <i>X</i> and <i>Y</i> chromosomes are cytologically homomorphic<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Deng, C.-L. L. et al. Karyotype of asparagus by physical mapping of 45S and 5S rDNA by FISH. J. Genet. &#xA;                           91, 209â212 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d43926e1503">19</a></sup>, and <i>YY</i> âsupermaleâ genotypes are viable and produce fertile pollen<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Rick, C. M. &amp; Hanna, G. C. Determination of Sex in Asparagus officinalis L. Am. J. Bot. &#xA;                           30, 711 (1943)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d43926e1511">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Sneep, J. The significance of andromonoecy for the breeding of Asparagus officinalis L. Euphytica &#xA;                           2, 224â228 (1953)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d43926e1514">21</a></sup>, suggesting a recent origin of dioecy and sex chromosomes in the genus.</p><p>Male and female organs are both initiated in developing garden asparagus flower buds, and differences in the timing of cessation of male pistil development and degeneration of female anthers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Caporali, E. et al. Development of male and female flower in Asparagus officinalis. Search for point of transition from hermaphroditic to unisexual developmental pathway. Sex. Plant. Reprod. &#xA;                           7, 239â249 (1994)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d43926e1521">22</a></sup> are suggestive of a two-gene sex determination system. In <i>XX</i> females, anther development ceases before pollen microsoporogenesis occurs and is marked by a degeneration of the tapetal layer. In males, the stylar tube and receptive stigma fail to fully form, resulting in a functionally male flower<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Caporali, E. et al. Development of male and female flower in Asparagus officinalis. Search for point of transition from hermaphroditic to unisexual developmental pathway. Sex. Plant. Reprod. &#xA;                           7, 239â249 (1994)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d43926e1528">22</a></sup>.</p><p>Rigorous testing of the one and two-gene models for sex determination requires comprehensive characterization of the sex determination locus and the function of genes that segregate perfectly with sex. To that end, we have generated an annotated genome assembly for a <i>YY</i> male garden asparagus genotype (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#Fig1">1</a>, Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#MOESM1">1</a>), and using resequencing data from additional <i>YY</i> male and <i>XX</i> female genotypes we have characterized the non-recombining portion of the <i>Y</i> chromosome (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#Fig1">1b</a>). Further, resequencing of deletion mutants exhibiting male-to-female and male-to-hermaphrodite conversions implicates at least two separate <i>Y</i>-specific genes suppressing female development and promoting male development, respectively. In contrast to other recent investigations of sex-determination in plants<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Boualem, A. et al. A cucurbit androecy gene reveals how unisexual flowers develop and dioecy emerges. Science &#xA;                           350, 688â691 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d43926e1561">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Akagi, T., Henry, I. M., Tao, R. &amp; Comai, L. A Y-chromosomeâencoded small RNA acts as a sex determinant in persimmons. Science &#xA;                           346, 646â650 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d43926e1564">12</a></sup>, our findings support the classical two-gene model of Westergaard<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Westergaard, M. The mechanism of sex determination in dioecious flowering plants. Adv. Genet. &#xA;                           9, 217â281 (1958)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d43926e1568">8</a></sup> and Charlesworth<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Charlesworth, B. &amp; Charlesworth, D. A model for the evolution of dioecy and gynodioecy. Am. Nat. &#xA;                           112, 975â997 (1978)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d43926e1572">4</a></sup> for the conversion of an autosomal chromosome pair to sex chromosomes in association with a transition from hermaphroditism to dioecy.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Fig. 1"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-01064-8/MediaObjects/41467_2017_1064_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-01064-8/MediaObjects/41467_2017_1064_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Genome assembly and identification of the sex-linked region. <b>a</b> The distribution of full-length <i>Gypsy</i> and <i>Copia-</i>class retroelements and genes along the ten <i>Asparagus officinalis</i> pseudomolecules in 1âMb bins. <b>b</b> The alignment of the optical map against the assembled sequence contigs across the non-recombining region of the <i>Y</i> chromosome. Gray bars connecting the two indicate optical map BspQI nick sites that align. Red bars indicate putative boundaries for the non-recombining region on the <i>Y</i> chromosome. Seven gene models within the contiguously assembled region are numbered with red dots above the sequence assembly. Six additional hemizygous gene models were placed in this recombination bin but are in contigs that could not be anchored onto the optical map (TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#Tab1">1</a>)</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                     </div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome assembly and <i>Y</i> chromosome annotation</h3><p>To generate a high quality reference assembly and annotation for the 1.3 Gb/1C garden asparagus genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Harkess, A. et al. Retrotransposon proliferation coincident with the evolution of dioecy in Asparagus. G3: Genes Genomes Genet. &#xA;                           6, 2679â2685 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d43926e1637">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Å tajner, N., Bohanec, B. &amp; Javornik, B. Genetic variability of economically important Asparagus species as revealed by genome size analysis and rDNA ITS polymorphisms. Plant Sci. &#xA;                           162, 931â937 (2002)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d43926e1640">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Arumuganathan, K. &amp; Earle, E. Nuclear DNA content of some important plant species. Plant Mol. Biol. Rep. &#xA;                           9, 208â218 (1991)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d43926e1643">23</a></sup>, a fully homozygous doubled haploid <i>YY</i> individual derived through anther culture was sequenced. We employed a suite of complementary technologies including a variety of short and long-read sequencing strategies, BioNano Genomics optical mapping, resequencing of a diversity panel of additional doubled haploid <i>YY</i> males and <i>XX</i> females, and mapping of recombination events in a population of double haploid <i>YY</i> and <i>XX</i> siblings derived from anther culture using a single <i>XY</i> male parent (Supplementary NotesÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#MOESM1">1</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#MOESM1">3</a>; Supplementary TablesÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#MOESM1">1</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#MOESM1">4</a>; Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#MOESM4">1</a>; Supplementary Figs.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#MOESM1">1</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#MOESM1">2</a>). Combining genetic and optical map data, 93.7% of the genome assembly was placed within recombination intervals on the 10 linkage groups in the genetic map, and 29.3% of the assembly was ordered and oriented within recombination intervals. Although the genome is largely comprised of recently-inserted Long Terminal Repeat (LTR) retrotransposons (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#MOESM1">1</a>), nearly 95% of the Core Eukaryotic Gene Model Annotations (CEGMA) and 88.2% of the BUSCO plant gene annotations were identified in the asparagus gene models. Sex-linked segregation patterns and a previously mapped <i>Y</i>-linked marker (Asp1-T7)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Jamsari, A., Nitz, I., Reamon-BÃ¼ttner, S. M. &amp; Jung, C. BAC-derived diagnostic markers for sex determination in asparagus. Theor. Appl. Genet. &#xA;                           108, 1140â1146 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d43926e1694">24</a></sup> identified a non-recombining region covering approximately one megabase on the 132.4âMb (â~â0.75%) <i>Y</i> chromosome. As seen in papaya<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Liu, Z. Y. et al. A primitive Y chromosome in papaya marks incipient sex chromosome evolution. Nature &#xA;                           427, 348â352 (2004)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d43926e1702">25</a></sup>, most of the non-recombining sex determination region of the <i>Y</i> chromosome is hemizygous with an increased density of retrotransposons compared to the surrounding pseudoautosomal region (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#Fig1">1a</a>). Twelve of thirteen gene predictions in the non-recombining sex determination region of the <i>YY</i> males are missing from <i>XX</i> females (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#Fig1">1b</a>; TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#Tab1">1</a>; Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#MOESM1">2</a>; Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#MOESM1">3</a>). Five of the predicted <i>Y</i>-linked gene model annotations do not have clear hits against the NCBI nr or Swiss-Prot databases; these gene models could represent either lineage-specific genes or artifacts of the annotation process.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Annotations of the 13 hemizygous non-recombining genes on the <i>Y</i> chromosome</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>Among the <i>Y</i>-specific genes with identified matches in other plant species is a single copy homolog of <i>DEFECTIVE IN TAPETUM DEVELOPMENT AND FUNCTION 1</i> (<i>TDF1</i>). Female organ development is unperturbed in <i>Arabidopsis TDF1</i> knockouts, but they do not produce pollen grains due to degeneration of the tapetum<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Zhu, J. et al. Defective in Tapetal Development and Function 1 is essential for anther development and tapetal function for microspore maturation in Arabidopsis. Plant J. &#xA;                           55, 266â277 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d43926e1968">26</a></sup>. In asparagus <i>XX</i> females, anthers typically abort shortly before pollen is formed, exhibiting a clear degeneration of the tapetal layer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Caporali, E. et al. Development of male and female flower in Asparagus officinalis. Search for point of transition from hermaphroditic to unisexual developmental pathway. Sex. Plant. Reprod. &#xA;                           7, 239â249 (1994)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d43926e1976">22</a></sup> and interruption of the microsporogenesis transcriptional program<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Harkess, A. et al. Sex-biased gene expression in dioecious garden asparagus (Asparagus officinalis). New Phytol. &#xA;                           207, 883â892 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d43926e1980">27</a></sup>. On the basis of its male-sterile knockout phenotype in <i>Arabidopsis</i>, the single copy, <i>Y</i>-specific <i>aspTDF1</i> is an obvious candidate for the sex-linked male promoting gene in garden asparagus. <i>AspTDF1</i> has also recently been confirmed as male-specific in all tested garden asparagus cultivars and other diecious <i>Asparagus</i> species closely related to garden asparagus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Murase, K. et al. MYB transcription factor gene involved in sex determination in Asparagus officinalis. Genes Cells &#xA;                           22, 115â123 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d43926e2000">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Tsugama, D. et al. A putative MYB35 ortholog is a candidate for the sex-determining genes in Asparagus officinalis. Sci. Rep. &#xA;                           7, 41497 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d43926e2003">29</a></sup>. Single-gene knockouts of all <i>Y</i>-linked genes would be necessary to rigorously test whether <i>aspTDF</i> is the one and only <i>Y</i>-specific promoter of male function in garden asparagus. The influence of other <i>Y</i>-specific genes, including a multi-copy ethylene response gene with an <i>APETALA 2 (AP2)</i> domain (gene model 1.235), is unknown.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Deletion mutants exhibiting male to female conversion</h3><p>Experimental mutants were generated through cobalt-60 gamma irradiation of <i>XY</i> male seeds derived from a cross of an <i>XX</i> seed parent with a <i>YY</i> pollen donor (Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#MOESM1">2</a>; Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#MOESM1">3</a>). Seeds were sown and flowers were phenotyped 18 months later. Plants producing fruits were genotyped using microsatellite markers to verify that they were <i>XY</i> products of the parental <i>XX</i>Ã<i>YY</i> cross. Three verified <i>XY</i> progeny that produced only female flowers were resequenced to an average depth of Ã10 (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#MOESM1">5</a>). In line with our prediction that the non-recombining region contains all genes necessary for sex determination in males, the entire non-recombining region of the <i>Y</i> chromosome was found to be independently deleted in all three mutants. Consistent with either a one-gene or multi-gene model for the genetic basis of sex determination in garden asparagus, these results indicate that the deleted portions of these <i>Y</i> chromosomes encode one or more factors that dominantly repress female development and promote male function.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Male to hermaphrodite conversion mutants</h3><p>Inspection of flowers from an additional berry-producing irradiation mutant revealed that all of its flowers were hermaphroditic. Resequencing of this individual indicated a localized deletion limited to a single <i>Y</i>-specific gene containing a <i>DUF247</i> domain (Domain of Unknown Function 247, PF03140) (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#Fig2">2</a>). In addition, an independently derived spontaneous <i>XY</i> male-to-hermaphrodite mutant was identified in a separate germplasm collection. RNA-seq analysis of this plant and targeted sequencing of the <i>Y</i>-linked <i>DUF247</i> domain-containing gene implicated a frameshift mutation as responsible for the sexual conversion in this second <i>XY</i> hermaphrodite (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#Fig2">2</a>; Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#MOESM1">2</a>; Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#MOESM1">4</a>). Altogether, these two independent mutants provide strong evidence for the <i>Y</i>-specific <i>DUF247</i> gene as responsible for female suppression; we have named this gene <i>SUPPRESSOR OF FEMALE FUNCTION (SOFF)</i>. Interestingly, a <i>DUF247</i> domain-containing gene has been identified as the male component of the self-incompatibility S-locus in perennial ryegrass<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Manzanares, C. et al. A gene encoding a DUF247 domain protein co- segregates with the S self-incompatibility locus in perennial ryegrass. Mol. Biol. Evol. &#xA;                           33, 1â40 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d43926e2121">30</a></sup>. Consistent with the two-gene model for the origin of dioecy and incipient sex chromosomes, neither hermaphroditic asparagus mutants displayed disruption of anther development (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#Fig2">2b</a>), strongly suggesting that at least one other <i>Y</i>-specific gene promotes male function in garden asparagus. As described above, we hypothesize <i>aspTDF1</i> as a male promoter but functional analysis of this and all other <i>Y</i>-specific genes is necessary to test whether introduction of <i>aspTDF1</i> into an <i>XX</i> genotype would be both necessary and sufficient for conversion from female to hermaphrodite flower development.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Fig. 2"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-01064-8/MediaObjects/41467_2017_1064_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-01064-8/MediaObjects/41467_2017_1064_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>Mutations in sex determination genes on the Y. <b>a</b> Read alignment coverage (read counts) across the SOFF and aspTDF1 gene models for an <i>XY</i> male (K323), a sibling XX female related to the <i>YY</i> assembled genome, and the gamma-irradiated G033 genotype. <b>b</b> Flowers from a spontaneous male-to-hermaphrodite mutant compared to wild-type male and female flowers (Line3). A frameshift mutation was found in the SOFF CDS sequence (Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#MOESM1">1</a>)</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Small RNAs exhibiting sex-biased expression</h3><p>Several recent studies have identified sex chromosome genes with floral function, including a sex-linked small RNA-producing gene that ultimately represses anther development in diecious persimmon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Akagi, T., Henry, I. M., Tao, R. &amp; Comai, L. A Y-chromosomeâencoded small RNA acts as a sex determinant in persimmons. Science &#xA;                           346, 646â650 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d43926e2186">12</a></sup>. Comparisons of small RNA abundance between male and female vegetative and reproductive tissues in garden asparagus show differential sRNA patterns between sexes, but none of the genes within the non-recombining region of the <i>Y</i> exhibit evidence of post-transcriptional regulation involving sRNAs (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#Fig3">3</a>; Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#MOESM1">4</a>; Supplementary Figs.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#MOESM1">6</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#MOESM1">8</a>; Supplementary TablesÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#MOESM1">8</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#MOESM1">12</a>; Supplementary DataÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#MOESM5">2</a>â<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#MOESM6">3</a>). Interestingly one of the 167 annotated miRNAs in the asparagus genome, a copy of miR535a, is transcribed from the hemizygous region of the <i>Y</i> chromosome. However, a second copy of the MIR535a locus was identified on chromosome 9. Consequently, mature miR535a transcripts were found in all tissues sampled from both males and females and there was no consistent pattern of sex-biased expression tested across three distinct garden asparagus lines (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#Fig3">3</a>; Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#MOESM1">4</a>). Six miRNAsâaof-miR167b, aof-miR2118a, aof-miR2118c, aof-miR2275a, aof-miR2275b, and aof-miR8026âdid exhibit consistent male-biased expression in spear tips sampled from all three garden asparagus lines (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#Fig3">3b</a>). As was seen in analysis of mRNA expression profiles<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Harkess, A. et al. Sex-biased gene expression in dioecious garden asparagus (Asparagus officinalis). New Phytol. &#xA;                           207, 883â892 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d43926e2231">27</a></sup>, however, sex-biased expression of miRNAs may be triggered at points in the anther or pistil developmental pathways that act downstream from the hypothesized sex determination genes <i>aspTDF1</i> and <i>SOFF</i>, respectively. At the same time, six of the microRNA gene families exhibiting sex-biased expression in flowers (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#Fig3">3a</a>)âmiR156, miR160, miR167, miR169, miR398 and miR399âalso exhibited sex-biased abundance in male and female poplar flowers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Song, Y., Ma, K., Ci, D., Zhang, Z. &amp; Zhang, D. Sexual dimorphism floral microRNA profiling and target gene expression in andromonoecious poplar (Populus tomentosa). PLoS ONE &#xA;                           8, e62681 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d43926e2245">31</a></sup>, raising the possibility of conserved sex-biased expression.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Fig. 3"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-01064-8/MediaObjects/41467_2017_1064_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-01064-8/MediaObjects/41467_2017_1064_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>miRNA expression variation across sexes and tissues. <b>a</b> Developmental profile of conserved and novel miRNAs (<i>n</i>=106) identified in garden asparagus. Novel miRNAs have identifiers starting miR8000 and above. The miRNAs are clustered to display tissue-type preferences. An asterisk marks where the cluster plot is split and continued. <b>b</b> miRNAs showing preferential expression patterns between XY male (M) and XX female (F) asparagus spears from three genotypes (8A, 8B, and 10) included in this study. The miRNAs displaying preferential expression (&gt;â=2 fold) in at least two spear genotypes are represented here. Higher values highlighted in red indicate enrichment in male spears, and green cells indicate female-biased expression</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Variation in the sex determination region over time</h3><p>The genus <i>Asparagus</i> includes just over 200 species, the vast majority of which are hermaphrodites<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Kubota, S., Konno, I. &amp; Kanno, A. Molecular phylogeny of the genus Asparagus (Asparagaceae) explains interspecific crossability between the garden asparagus (A. officinalis) and other Asparagus species. Theor. Appl. Genet. &#xA;                           124, 345â354 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d43926e2289">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="A. Kanno, J. Yokoyama, âAsparagusâ in Wild Crop Relatives: Genomic and Breeding Resources (ed C. Kole) Chapter 3 (Springer-Verlag Berlin Heidelberg, 2011)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d43926e2292">18</a></sup>. Previous work has shown that dioecy arose within a single clade in the <i>Asparagus</i> phylogeny<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Kubota, S., Konno, I. &amp; Kanno, A. Molecular phylogeny of the genus Asparagus (Asparagaceae) explains interspecific crossability between the garden asparagus (A. officinalis) and other Asparagus species. Theor. Appl. Genet. &#xA;                           124, 345â354 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d43926e2299">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Norup, M. F. et al. Evolution of Asparagus L. (Asparagaceae): Out-of-South-Africa and multiple origins of sexual dimorphism. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. &#xA;                           92, 25â44 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d43926e2302">15</a></sup>. To assess whether <i>SOFF</i> and <i>aspTDF1</i> arose as male-specific sex determination genes with the origin of dioecy in <i>Asparagus</i>, we performed additional whole genome resequencing on an <i>XY</i> male and <i>XX</i> female <i>Asparagus cochinchinensis</i> accessions (Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#MOESM1">3</a>; Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#MOESM1">6</a>). The <i>A. cochinchinensis</i> and <i>A. officinalis</i> lineages diverged very early within the diecious <i>Asparagus</i> clade<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Kubota, S., Konno, I. &amp; Kanno, A. Molecular phylogeny of the genus Asparagus (Asparagaceae) explains interspecific crossability between the garden asparagus (A. officinalis) and other Asparagus species. Theor. Appl. Genet. &#xA;                           124, 345â354 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d43926e2341">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Norup, M. F. et al. Evolution of Asparagus L. (Asparagaceae): Out-of-South-Africa and multiple origins of sexual dimorphism. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. &#xA;                           92, 25â44 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d43926e2344">15</a></sup>. In addition, hermaphroditic <i>Asparagus virgatus</i> was resequenced, and reads from both species were aligned to the <i>A. officinalis</i> reference genome. A <i>SOFF</i> ortholog was identified in the <i>XY</i> male <i>A. cochinchinensis</i> genotype but not in the <i>XX</i> female genotype. Ortholog identification was based on the observation that the putatively <i>Y</i>-specific <i>DUF247</i> genes in <i>A. cochinchinensis</i> and <i>A. officinalis</i> both have longer open reading frames in intron 1, and further phylogenetic analysis of all sampled <i>SOFF/DUF247</i> homologs placed the putative <i>A. cochinchinensis SOFF</i> ortholog as sister to the <i>A. officinalis SOFF</i> gene (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#Fig4">4</a>, Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#MOESM1">2</a>). These findings imply that a gene duplication event set the stage for the evolution of the female suppressing function and the origin of dioecy in <i>Asparagus</i>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Fig. 4"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-01064-8/MediaObjects/41467_2017_1064_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-01064-8/MediaObjects/41467_2017_1064_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>Polyploidy and the evolution of sex determination genes. <b>a</b> Synteny dotplot between chromosome <i>Y</i> and chromosome 5. <b>b</b> Gene tree of homologous SOFF gene contigs identified in hermaphroditic <i>A. virgatus</i> and a male and female accession of <i>A. cochinchinensis</i>. The gene and species tree were manually reconciled to identify speciation nodes versus gene duplication nodes</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>Interestingly, <i>A. cochinchinensis</i> reads from both male and female genotypes mapped across the <i>aspTDF1</i> exons and no nucleotide differences were observed between reads derived from the male and female genotypes. This finding suggests that the <i>aspTDF1</i> ortholog resides in a recombining portion of the <i>A. cochinchinensis</i> genome, and unlike <i>SOFF</i>, <i>aspTDF1</i> may not have contributed to the origin of dioecy within <i>Asparagus</i>. The PCR-based survey of Murase et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Tsugama, D. et al. A putative MYB35 ortholog is a candidate for the sex-determining genes in Asparagus officinalis. Sci. Rep. &#xA;                           7, 41497 (2017)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d43926e2458">29</a></sup> also found evidence for full length <i>aspTDF1</i> genes in females of <i>A. cochinchinensis</i> and <i>A. stipularis</i>, two diecious <i>Asparagus</i> species that are distantly related to garden asparagus within the diecious <i>Asparagus</i> clade<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Kubota, S., Konno, I. &amp; Kanno, A. Molecular phylogeny of the genus Asparagus (Asparagaceae) explains interspecific crossability between the garden asparagus (A. officinalis) and other Asparagus species. Theor. Appl. Genet. &#xA;                           124, 345â354 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d43926e2478">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Norup, M. F. et al. Evolution of Asparagus L. (Asparagaceae): Out-of-South-Africa and multiple origins of sexual dimorphism. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. &#xA;                           92, 25â44 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d43926e2481">15</a></sup>. It is possible that <i>aspTDF1</i> is functionally silenced in <i>A. cochinchinensis</i> and <i>A. stipularis</i> females. For example, epigenetic silencing of the <i>CmWIP1</i> transcription factor gene in melon (<i>Cucumis melo</i>) has been shown to promote production of female flowers<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Martin, A. et al. A transposon-induced epigenetic change leads to sex determination in melon. Nature &#xA;                           461, 1135â1138 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d43926e2501">32</a></sup>. In any case, it seems that recombination is impeding divergence of <i>aspTDF1</i> alleles sampled in the resequenced <i>A. cochinchinensis</i> male and female genotypes, suggesting that the male-specific non-recombining portion of the <i>Y</i>-chromosome in garden asparagus has either expanded to include <i>aspTDF1</i>, or <i>aspTDF1</i> has been secondarily recruited from elsewhere in the genome since the origin of a proto-<i>Y</i> chromosome in the last common ancestor of all diecious <i>Asparagus</i> species.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Major polyploidy events predate dioecy in <i>Asparagus</i>
                        </h3><p>The haploid genome size of diecious <i>Asparagus</i> species has been characterized as being nearly double that of hermaphroditic species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Harkess, A. et al. Retrotransposon proliferation coincident with the evolution of dioecy in Asparagus. G3: Genes Genomes Genet. &#xA;                           6, 2679â2685 (2016)." href="#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d43926e2542">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Å tajner, N., Bohanec, B. &amp; Javornik, B. Genetic variability of economically important Asparagus species as revealed by genome size analysis and rDNA ITS polymorphisms. Plant Sci. &#xA;                           162, 931â937 (2002)." href="#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d43926e2542_1">17</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="A. Kanno, J. Yokoyama, âAsparagusâ in Wild Crop Relatives: Genomic and Breeding Resources (ed C. Kole) Chapter 3 (Springer-Verlag Berlin Heidelberg, 2011)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d43926e2545">18</a></sup>. Earlier work suggested that the increased genome size may have been due to increased rates of retrotransposon accumulation in diecious <i>Asparagus</i> genomes, but synteny analysis of the reference genome assembly revealed evidence for at least two ancient whole genome duplications (WGDs; Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#Fig5">5</a>). Phylogenomic analyses were performed in order to assess the timing of these WGD events in relationship to duplication events in the <i>DUF247</i> gene family (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#Fig4">4</a>) and the origin of dioecy in the <i>Asparagus</i> genus. All asparagus gene models were clustered into gene families with genes from 15 other sequenced flowering plant genomes (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#MOESM1">7</a>). Transcript assemblies for hermaphroditic species <i>Asparagus asparagoides</i>, <i>Yucca aloifolia</i> and <i>Acorus americanus</i> were sorted into these gene family clusters using BLAST. Phylogenetic analyses of the resulting gene family circumscriptions indicated that duplicates mapping to syntenic blocks within the garden asparagus genome were found to have diverged before the origin of <i>Asparagus</i>, and well before the origin of dioecy within the genus. Reconciliation of gene trees and species trees placed two WGD events within the Asparagales lineage following divergence from the Orchidaceae: Asparagales-Î±, prior to the divergence of <i>A. officinalis</i> and <i>A. asparagoides</i> and another event, Asparagales-Î², predating the divergence of <i>Yucca</i> and <i>Asparagus</i> (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#Fig5">5</a>). Phylogenetic analyses of homeologs on the <i>Y</i> chromosome and a syntenic portion of chromosome 5 (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#Fig4">4</a>) associated their duplication with the Asparagales-Î± WGD, prior to the <i>DUF247</i> duplication that spawned <i>SOFF</i>, before the origin of dioecy within <i>Asparagus</i>, and before geographic expansion of the genus from southern Africa to northern Africa, Europe and Asia<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Kubota, S., Konno, I. &amp; Kanno, A. Molecular phylogeny of the genus Asparagus (Asparagaceae) explains interspecific crossability between the garden asparagus (A. officinalis) and other Asparagus species. Theor. Appl. Genet. &#xA;                           124, 345â354 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d43926e2612">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Norup, M. F. et al. Evolution of Asparagus L. (Asparagaceae): Out-of-South-Africa and multiple origins of sexual dimorphism. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. &#xA;                           92, 25â44 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d43926e2615">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="A. Kanno, J. Yokoyama, âAsparagusâ in Wild Crop Relatives: Genomic and Breeding Resources (ed C. Kole) Chapter 3 (Springer-Verlag Berlin Heidelberg, 2011)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d43926e2618">18</a></sup>.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Fig. 5"><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-01064-8/MediaObjects/41467_2017_1064_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41467-017-01064-8/MediaObjects/41467_2017_1064_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>Mapping whole genome duplication events.
