All relationships other than the node joining the two Alismatales species, <i>Spirodella polyrhiza</i> and <i>Zostera marina</i> are supported with bootstrap support values &gt;â95%. Branch coloring represents the number of gene trees analyzed in this study that support WGDs in the ancestors of at least two terminal taxa</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div>
                        <p>Broader phylogenomic analysis of duplication events across the sampled species refined the placement of ancient WGD events that had been inferred in previous analyses of monocot genomes (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#Fig5">5</a>). Multiple putative WGD events were identified across the taxa sampled for our phylogenomic analysis, including both previously identified and novel events. Gene family trees were queried to estimate the timing of duplication for synteny-derived paralog pairs identified in <i>A. officinalis</i> (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#MOESM1">5</a>). Of these trees, 1149 were found to contain syntelog pairs within the garden asparagus genome. Known WGD events were identified as unique subtrees within these 1149 gene trees, including (as numbered in Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#Fig5">5</a>): Poaceaeâ(1) <i>rho</i> (433 gene tree clades with Bootstrap Values of 80% or greater (BSV 80)), Poalesâ (2) <i>sigma</i> (78 BSV 80), monocotsâ (11) <i>tau</i> (56 BSV 80), Orchidaceaeâ (10) orchid WGD (336 BSV 80), and eudicotsâ (19) <i>gamma</i> (212 BSV 80) (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#Fig5">5</a>). The two events inferred within the Asparagaceae were Asparagales-Î² shared with <i>Yucca</i> (8) (135 BSV 80) and Asparagales-Î± just predating divergence of the two included <i>Asparagus</i> species (7) (262 BSV 80) (Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#Fig5">5</a>). More work is necessary to properly model the gene loss process following whole genome duplications in order to rigorously test whether the clear patterns seen in our synteny and phylogenomic analyses can be confidently attributed to genome duplications. Nonetheless, both synteny and gene tree analyses clearly indicate that the two rounds of duplication within the Asparagales lineage leading to <i>Asparagus</i> significantly predated the origin of dioecy within the genus.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><p>Taken together, the body of data presented here strongly supports that the evolution of an active <i>Y</i> chromosome in <i>Asparagus</i> was mediated by gene duplication and neofuntionalization of a female suppressor and subsequent expansion of the non-recombining sex determination region as predicted in the two-gene model<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Charlesworth, B. &amp; Charlesworth, D. A model for the evolution of dioecy and gynodioecy. Am. Nat. &#xA;                           112, 975â997 (1978)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d43926e2771">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Jones, D. F. Unisexual maize plants and their bearing on sex differentiation in other plants and in animals. Genetics &#xA;                           19, 552â567 (1934)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d43926e2774">10</a></sup>. Given the immense diversity in sexual systems across the angiosperms and numerous independent origins of dioecy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Ming, R., Bendahmane, A. &amp; Renner, S. S. Sex chromosomes in land plants. Annu. Rev. Plant. Biol. &#xA;                           62, 485â514 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d43926e2778">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Renner, S. S. The relative and absolute frequencies of angiosperm sexual systems: dioecy, monoecy, gynodioecy, and an updated online database. Am. J. Bot. &#xA;                           101, 1588â1596 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d43926e2781">7</a></sup>, we expect different genetic factors will be regulating male and female function in unrelated dioecious lineages. The work done to date suggests that single-gene sex determination systems may evolve following fixation of null mutations in unlinked but interacting sex-specification genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Westergaard, M. The mechanism of sex determination in dioecious flowering plants. Adv. Genet. &#xA;                           9, 217â281 (1958)." href="#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d43926e2785">8</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Boualem, A. et al. A cucurbit androecy gene reveals how unisexual flowers develop and dioecy emerges. Science &#xA;                           350, 688â691 (2015)." href="#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d43926e2785_1">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Jones, D. F. Unisexual maize plants and their bearing on sex differentiation in other plants and in animals. Genetics &#xA;                           19, 552â567 (1934)." href="#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d43926e2785_2">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Dellaporta, S. L. &amp; Calderon-Urrea, A. The sex determination process in maize. Science &#xA;                           266, 1501â1505 (1994)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d43926e2788">11</a></sup>, or as we show here for garden asparagus, two or more linked genes may act independently during development of female or male reproductive pathways.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Genome sequencing and initial assembly</h3><p>A doubled haploid <i>YY</i> genotype (DH00/086) was generated through anther culture<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Qiao, Y. M. &amp; Falavigna, A. An improved in vitro anther culture method for obtaining doubled-haploid clones of asparagus. Acta Hort. &#xA;                           271, 145â150 (1990)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d43926e2807">33</a></sup>. Nearly 341 Gb of a variety of Illumina reads were generated for DH00/086, utilizing insert sizes that ranged from short insert paired-end libraries (170, 200, 500, 800, 2000ânt) to larger mate-pair libraries (5, 10, 20, 40âkb). An initial assembly was produced using SOAPdenovo2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience &#xA;                           1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d43926e2811">34</a></sup>, gap-filled with GapCloser (<a href="http://sourceforge.net/projects/soapdenovo2/files/GapCloser/">http://sourceforge.net/projects/soapdenovo2/files/GapCloser/</a>), and further scaffolded with SSPACE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Boetzer, M., Henkel, C. V., Jansen, H. J., Butler, D. &amp; Pirovano, W. Scaffolding pre-assembled contigs using SSPACE. Bioinformatics &#xA;                           27, 578â579 (2011)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d43926e2822">35</a></sup>. Additional detail is provided in Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#MOESM1">1</a>.</p><p>To improve the contiguity of the Illumina-based SOAPdenovo assembly, we generated 6.07Gb of Pacific Biosystems (PacBio) long-reads for further gap-filling and scaffolding. High molecular weight DNA isolated from the reference DH00/086 individual was used as input for library preparation, size selected for &gt;â20âkb fragments using the BluePippin (Sage Science, Beverly, MA) and sequenced on a PacBio RS II. PBJelly2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="English, A. C. et al. Mind the Gap: upgrading genomes with pacific biosciences RS long-read sequencing technology. PLoS ONE &#xA;                           7, 1â12 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d43926e2833">36</a></sup>; <a href="https://sourceforge.net/projects/pb-jelly/">https://sourceforge.net/projects/pb-jelly/</a>) version PBSuite_14.7.14 was used to improve the existing scaffold assembly with parameters â-minMatch 8 -sdpTupleSize 8 -minPctIdentity 75 -bestn 5 -nCandidates 10 -maxScore â500 ânoSplitSubreadsâ, followed by an additional run of GapCloser. Additional detail is provided in Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#MOESM1">1</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec12">Genetic mapping</h3><p>To anchor the assembly onto pseudomolecules representing the 10 haploid chromosomes in garden asparagus, we utilized a population of 74 doubled haploid individuals to generate a genetic map by low depth (Ã3.5 average depth of coverage) resequencing. The doubled haploid individuals, 35 <i>XX</i> females and 39 <i>YY</i> supermales, were all derived from anther culture in a single <i>XY</i> male that was also sequenced. Additionally, the doubled haploid male and female parents of that <i>XY</i> male (i.e., grandparents of the double haploid progeny array) were also resequenced, so that all possible segregating alleles could be identified.</p><p>The 77 doubled haploid offspring were resequenced to an average of Ã3.5 coverage using Illumina PE150 reads. All reads were aligned to the contigs using BWA version 0.7.10-r789 with default parameters. Samtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Li, H. et al. The sequence alignment/map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics &#xA;                           25, 2078â2079 (2009)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d43926e2870">37</a></sup> version 1.2 mpileup was used to combine the results for all individuals, retaining the read depth for each allele at each locus (-t DP) and filtered for polymorphic SNPs using bcftools version 1.2. SNPs with average read depth of &lt;â1 across the population, indels, SNPs with &gt;â2 alleles and SNPs with fewer than 5 non-reference sequence reads across the whole population were removed using vcftools v0.1.12a. Genotypes for each individual were called as AA, AB, BB, or missing data from the mpileup output. Preliminary SNPs were further filtered by removing SNPs where the genotype quality score was &lt;â900, had &gt;â4 heterozygous individual scores called out of 77 individuals, or had a combined read depth &lt;â120 or &gt;â500 (average Ã1.66 and Ã6.95 coverage) across all individuals. The filtered variant set includes 3,352,321 SNPs, or an average of one SNP per 343âbp.</p><p>SNPs from the same sequence contig were combined to create a consensus genotype for each contig, using the rule that a consensus genotype was assigned if &gt;â90% of the individual SNPs not including missing data from a contig had the same genotype. A preliminary map was first generated using contigs with &gt;â100 SNPs and progressively refined using contigs with fewer SNPs. The genetic map was constructed and curated within Microsoft Excel, as described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Bowers, J. E. et al. Development of an ultra-dense genetic map of the sunflower genome based on single-feature polymorphisms. PLoS ONE &#xA;                           7, 721â729 (2012)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d43926e2877">38</a></sup>. The resulting genetic map of 649 distinct recombination patterns assembled into the expected 10 linkage groups, and any sequence scaffold could also be oriented relative to the genetic map if the scaffold spanned a recombination event for one or more individual in the mapping population.</p><p>As some of the regions of interest, notably the sex-determination region, could have corresponded to large indels between the maternal and paternal haplotypes (i.e., hemizygous), scaffolds from these regions would not contain SNPs, and could not be placed on a SNP-based genetic map. We used sequence depth of coverage as a QTL to aid mapping of 9336 contigs that were identified with segments segregating for depth of coverage in the mapping population. Additional detail is provided in Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#MOESM1">1</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec13">Assembly correction and super-scaffolding with optical maps</h3><p>The genetic mapping data were used to identify chimeric contigs or scaffolds in the genome assembly resulting from mis-assembly. Contigs with five or more consecutive SNPs that mapped to two different genetic loci &gt;â5âcm apart were cut into separate contigs. Scaffolds that contained contigs mapping to different loci were broken. A total of 2864 chimeric joins between contigs within assembled sequence scaffolds were identified and placed in the proper recombination bin.</p><p>The scaffolds from the sequence genome assembly were mapped onto the optical map contigs using the Comparison tool in Irysview software (BioNano Genomics; Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#Fig1">1b</a>, Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#MOESM1">1</a>). The resulting super-scaffolds were then manually edited to remove sequence contigs where the genetic map data conflicted with the optical map assignments. In cases where 2 or more sequence contigs mapped to the same part of an optical map, the sequence contig with the lower e-value was removed. In total 80.4% of the sequence assembly could be placed on the optical maps, and the remaining contigs were generally too small to contain enough restriction sites to assign a location. The second optical map of a sibling doubled haploid <i>XX</i> female asparagus plant was used to order and orient a small fraction (&lt;â1%) of the genome that was genetically mapped but not oriented by the male optical map and genetic SNP map. Ordered and oriented segments were put together as contiguous super-scaffolds within each recombination interval. Unordered scaffolds were placed within the genetic map at the distal end of each recombination interval. Only 6.2% of the genome assembly could not be assigned to chromosomal locations in the linkage map. Additional detail is provided in Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#MOESM1">1</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec14">Repetitive element annotation</h3><p>LTRharvest (GenomeTools v1.5.1) was used to annotate full-length LTR retrotransposons using default parameters except for â-similar 55 âmaxdistltr 40000â to identify older insertions and longer elements. A total of 57,045 full-length LTR retrotransposons were annotated with LTRharvest and classified as either <i>Gypsy</i> or <i>Copia</i> using RepeatClassifier. The 57,045 full-length LTR retrotransposons were additionally annotated using the RepeatClassifier program (v1.0.8), part of the RepeatModeler package.</p><p>We also utilized RepeatMasker with a custom transposon database with options âRepeatMasker -e wublast -pa 10 -lib Asparagus.repeatexplorer.annotatedRepeats.fa -no_is -html âgffâ. The custom transposon database was derived from clustering and annotation of low coverage whole genome shotgun 454 reads<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Harkess, A. et al. Retrotransposon proliferation coincident with the evolution of dioecy in Asparagus. G3: Genes Genomes Genet. &#xA;                           6, 2679â2685 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d43926e2927">16</a></sup> using the RepeatExplorer pipeline, freely available in Dryad (<a href="http://doi.org/10.5061/dryad.1k450">http://doi.org/10.5061/dryad.1k450</a>). In total, 74.08% of the genome was masked using the custom repeat database.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec15">Gene annotation</h3><p>We leveraged a suite of ab initio and evidence-based programs for gene annotation. RNA-Seq reads from Harkess et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Harkess, A. et al. Sex-biased gene expression in dioecious garden asparagus (Asparagus officinalis). New Phytol. &#xA;                           207, 883â892 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d43926e2947">27</a></sup> of vegetative shoot and reproductive spear tip tissue, in addition to root tissue (Biosample SAMN05366843) were individually aligned to the genome using TopHat2 v2.0.14 with the fragment size set to 250âbp and std. deviation to 100âbp. The min and max intron size were set to 50 and 25,000âbp, respectively. Each of the mapped RNA-Seq libraries were then processed using the Cufflinks transcript assembler (<a href="http://cole-trapnell-lab.github.io/cufflinks/,%20ver.%202.2.1">http://cole-trapnell-lab.github.io/cufflinks/, ver. 2.2.1</a>). Multiple Cufflinks assemblies were then merged using Cuffmerge application to produce a combined cufflinks transcriptome assembly. A total of 81,549 transcripts were identified in the combined assembly.</p><p>A comprehensive transcriptome database was built using de novo and genome-guided RNA-Seq assembly. The PASA pipelines (<a href="http://pasapipeline.github.io">http://pasapipeline.github.io</a>) <i>hybrid approach to transcript reconstruction</i> protocol was used to construct the transcriptome database. The de novo and genome-guided RNA-Seq assembly was performed using Trinity RNA-Seq assembler (Ver. 2.0.2). The de novo assembly was done with in-silico normalization set to Ã50 coverage. The combined Cufflinks assembly was also used. The final transcript count in the database was 88,465 genes.</p><p>The Augustus ab initio gene finder (<a href="http://bioinf.uni-greifswald.de/augustus/%20ver.%202.5.5">http://bioinf.uni-greifswald.de/augustus/ ver. 2.5.5</a>) was used to identify gene models. The gene finder was trained using 500 full length gene models identified from the PASA built transcriptome database. A total of 27,334 genes were identified by Augustus. The SNAP (Semi-HMM-based Nucleic Acid Parser, version 2006-07-28) ab initio gene finder was also used to identify gene models. The gene finder was trained using the same 500 full length gene models from the PASA built transcriptome database. A total of 17,096 gene models were identified by SNAP.</p><p>To leverage annotations from additional genomes to provide additional evidence for asparagus gene models, we aligned protein models from five species using Exonerate version 2.2.0: <i>Arabidopsis thaliana, Musa acuminata, Oryza sativa, Phalaenopsis equestris</i>, and <i>Vitis vinifera</i>
                           <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="DâHont, A. et al. The banana (Musa acuminata) genome and the evolution of monocotyledonous plants. Nature &#xA;                           488, 213â217 (2012)." href="#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d43926e2990">39</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="The Arabidopsis Genome Initiative. Nature &#xA;                           408, 796â815 (2000)." href="#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d43926e2990_1">40</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="International Rice Genome Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature &#xA;                           436, 793â800 (2005)." href="#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d43926e2990_2">41</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Cai, J. et al. The genome sequence of the orchid Phalaenopsis equestris. Nat. We combined evidence from RNA Seq, <i>ab initio</i> and homology-based approaches utilizing Evidence Modeler version 1.1.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Haas, B. Genome Biol. &#xA;                           9, R7 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d43926e3000">44</a></sup> to predict 27,656 genes which were used for all downstream analysis. These gene models were compared against the Core Eukaryotic Gene Model Annotation (CEGMA)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K. &amp; Korf, I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics &#xA;                           23, 1061â1067 (2007)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d43926e3005">45</a></sup> v2.5 and the BUSCO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="SimÃ£o, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics &#xA;                           31, 3210â3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d43926e3009">46</a></sup> Plant Gene databases to assess completeness of the annotation.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec16">Female suppressing gene identification</h3><p>To identify a female suppressing gene on the <i>Y</i> chromosome, we identified and sequenced several independent hermaphrodite lines that were each derived from a different <i>XY</i> male. Nearly 40,000 <i>XY</i> seeds were treated with cobalt-60 gamma irradiation to identify a single field-grown hermaphrodite mutant (G033) for whole genome resequencing (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#MOESM1">3</a>). A wild type <i>XY</i> untreated control plant (K323) was also resequenced. Additional gamma irradiation produced 3 more male-to-female mutants (Lim_mut1, Lim_mut3, and Lim_mut4), and a control <i>XY</i> male plant (Lim_mut5) was also resequenced. Further, a spontaneous <i>XY</i> male-to-hermaphrodite mutant (Line3) was discovered in a glasshouse. Floral bud RNA-seq of this individual, followed by genomic PCR-based confirmation, revealed a single base pair deletion in a coding exon of the <i>SOFF</i> female suppressor gene that results in a frameshift mutation and a premature stop codon. Additional detail regarding the refinement of the non-recombining region assembly as well as mutant identification is provided in Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#MOESM1">2</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec17">Small RNA annotation and analysis</h3><p>We generated and analyzed small RNA (sRNA) libraries from different organs and developmental stages. <i>Asparagus officinalis</i> samples were collected from a commercial field in the T.S. Smith and Sonâs Farm (<a href="http://www.tssmithandsons.com/">http://www.tssmithandsons.com/</a>), Bridgeville, Delaware, as well as from female, male, and supermale whole spear tip tissue from a previous study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Harkess, A. et al. Sex-biased gene expression in dioecious garden asparagus (Asparagus officinalis). New Phytol. &#xA;                           207, 883â892 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d43926e3067">27</a></sup>. Samples were collected and anthers were dissected using a 2âmm stage micrometer (Wards Science, cat. #949910) in a stereo microscope, and immediately frozen in liquid nitrogen until total RNA isolation was performed. Total RNA was isolated using the PureLink Plant RNA Reagent (ThermoFisher Scientific, cat. #12322012) following the manufacturerâs instructions. Small RNAs (20â30ânt) were size selected in a 15% polyacrylamide/urea gel and used for small RNA library preparation using the Illumina TruSeq Small RNA Prep Kit. Detailed description of analysis can be found in Supplementary NoteÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#MOESM1">4</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec18">Assembly of <i>SOFF</i> across <i>Asparagus</i> species</h3><p>To identify variation in the Y chromosome across additional species in the genus, we resequenced the hermaphroditic <i>A. virgatus</i> and a male and female accession of diecious <i>A. cochinchinensis</i> each to &gt;â20X coverage with single end 150ânt reads on an Illumina NextSeq500. To in silico assemble <i>SOFF</i> homologs across these individuals, SE150 reads were aligned to the genome with bwa mem v0.7.5a. Using bedtools v2.25.0, we extracted every read that aligned to the <i>SOFF</i> and <i>aspTDF1</i> gene model exons with at least 1nt overlap. Extracted reads were then assembled for each exon in cap3 (v. 12/21/07), requiring a 98% overlap identity over at least 40nt. Contigs were then aligned using PASTA (<a href="https://github.com/smirarab/pasta">https://github.com/smirarab/pasta</a>) and a RAxML tree was generated using the CAT model within PASTA.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec19">Synteny and phylogenomic analysis</h3><p>We performed pairwise genome alignments between a total of 12 selected genomes, including <i>Amborella</i> (<i>Amborella trichocarpa</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Amborella Genome Consortium. The banana (Musa acuminata) genome and the evolution of monocotyledonous plants. The Spirodela polyrhiza genome reveals insights into its neotenous reduction fast growth and aquatic lifestyle. Nat. Oil palm genome sequence reveals divergence of interfertile species in Old and New worlds. Nature &#xA;                           500, 335â339 (2013)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d43926e3163">50</a></sup>, orchid (<i>Phalaenopsis equestris</i>)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Cai, J. et al. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. For each pairwise genome alignments, the coding sequences of predicted gene models are compared to each other using LAST (last.cbrc.jp). Our synteny search pipeline defines syntenic blocks by chaining the LAST hits with a distance cutoff of 20 genes apart, also requiring at least 4 gene pairs per syntenic block (Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#MOESM1">5</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec20">Gene family circumscription and gene tree estimation</h3><p>Gene models from 14 genomes (Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#MOESM1">7</a>) were clustered into gene families using OrthoFinder ver. 0.4<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Emms, D. M. &amp; Kelly, S. OrthoFinder: solving fundamental biases in whole genome comparisons dramatically improves orthogroup inference accuracy. Genome Biol. &#xA;                           16, 157 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d43926e3221">54</a></sup>. Gene models from the <i>Zostera marina</i> and <i>Dendrobium catenatum</i>
                           <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Zhang, G.-Q. et al. The Dendrobium catenatum Lindl. genome sequence provides insights into polysaccharide synthase, floral development and adaptive evolution. Sci. Rep. &#xA;                           6, 19029 (2016)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d43926e3231">55</a></sup> genomes and transcriptome assemblies for <i>Acorus americanus</i>, <i>Yucca aloifolia</i> and <i>Asparagus asparagoides</i> were sorted into the gene family clusters based on best BLASTX (NCBI blast+ ver. 2.2.29) matches to the gene models in the OrthoFinder circumscribed gene families.</p><p>Inferred peptide sequences for each gene family cluster with more than five genes were using MAFFT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Katoh, K. &amp; Toh, H. Recent developments in the MAFFT multiple sequence alignment program. Brief Bioinform. &#xA;                           9, 286â298 (2008)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d43926e3248">56</a></sup> v7.215 and coding nucleotide sequences were codon aligned on top of the amino acid alignments using pal2nal<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Suyama, M., Torrents, D. &amp; Bork, P. PAL2NAL: robust conversion of protein sequence alignments into the corresponding codon alignments. Nucleic Acids Res. &#xA;                           34, W609âW612 (2006)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d43926e3252">57</a></sup>. Gene trees were estimated from the resulting codon alignments using the GTRGAMMA model in RAxML ver. 8.2.4<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML version 8: a tool for phylogenetic analysis and post-analysis of large phylogenies. Bioinformatics &#xA;                           30, 1312â1313 (2014)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d43926e3256">58</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec21">Phylogenomic placement of whole genome duplication events</h3><p>The timing of whole genome duplication events relative to branching events in the species tree were estimated using the PUG algorithm and software (<a href="https://github.com/mrmckain/PUG">https://github.com/mrmckain/PUG</a>). A species tree estimated based on coalescence analysis of 1,584 gene families that were found in to exist in one and only one copy in at least 13 of the 15 genomes clustered or sorted into gene families. Multi-gene bootstrap analyses were performed in ASTRAL ver. 4.10.0<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Mirarab, S. &amp; Warnow, T. ASTRAL-II: coalescent-based species tree estimation with many hundreds of taxa and thousands of genes. Bioinformatics &#xA;                           31, i44âi52 (2015)." href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d43926e3276">59</a></sup> to estimate relationships among all of the species listed in Supplementary TableÂ <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#MOESM1">7</a>. The resulting species tree was used to infer the timing of ancient whole genome duplications (WGDs) evident in syntenic blocks (see Supplementary Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#MOESM1">5</a>) by assessing the timing of duplication events in multi-copy gene trees.</p><p>To infer the timing of WGDs, the coalescence node for each paralogs pair in each rooted multi-copy gene tree was identified and species composition of the paralog subtree above each duplication node was compared against the species tree to identify the lower bound on timing of the gene duplication event in the species tree. The node of paralogs coalescence in the gene tree represents the timing of gene duplication and in whole genome duplication, the point of genome duplication and divergence. The lineage sister to the paralog subtree was also queried for species composition to provide additional support for the lower bound of the duplication event verifying adequate sampling of the species tree in the gene tree. If the timing of a gene duplication was mapped to a single internal branch in the species tree, bootstrap values for the coalescence node in the gene tree was recorded. The timing of WGDs were inferred based on the number of gene duplications mapping to each internal node on the species tree. Possible gene duplications on terminal branches were not considered since paralogs are difficult to distinguish from single locus isoforms. Figures representing the placement of gene duplications were made using the PUG_Figure_Maker.R script available in the PUG software package (<a href="https://github.com/mrmckain/PUG">https://github.com/mrmckain/PUG</a>). When displaying duplicated genes on species trees, only nodes with at least 10% of the maximum number of duplicated genes found at a node are shown in Fig.Â <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41467-017-01064-8#Fig5">5</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--divider" id="Sec22">Data availability</h3><p>All sequence data have been deposited in NCBIâs reference genome database (BioProject Accessions PRJNA317340 (genome shotgun reads), PRJNA259909 (RNA-Seq), PRJNA326431 (Asparagus asparaoidies transcriptome). Small RNA and PARE data are available at https://mpss.danforthcenter.org/dbs/index.php?SITE=asparagus_sRNA and https://mpss.danforthcenter.org/dbs/index.php?SITE=asparagus_pare. Gene alignments and trees are available at https://github.com/alexharkess/asparagus_genome. The genome is also available through CoGe (genomevolution.org/coge/OrganismView.pl?dsgid=33908) and bulk downloads of all data are available through <a href="http://asparagus.uga.edu/">http://asparagus.uga.edu/</a> along with a genome browser and BLAST portal. The authors declare that all other data supporting the findings of this study are available from the corresponding author upon request.</p></div></div></section>
